O, sons, since I have completed for you this book, which is the last of the science of medicine, through its completion reaching its end, and through its expositions and its explanations, it seems to me that I should complement it for you with this treatise, which is about the operation by hand, that is, surgery.
Because the operation by hand is laid low in our region and in our time, completely deprived, until the science of it is greatly endangered, and the trace of it is removed; and only a few small descriptions remain in the books of the Ancients, which mostly changed and turned into errors and doubts, so that their understandings are closed and the strength and art of it is extended away.
And it seems to me that I should revive it with the organization of this treatise in that way through exposition and explanation and abbreviation; and so that it comes with the forms of the cautery tools and other instruments of the work as well as with the addition of explanation, and with preparation of it, which is necessary.
But the reason why no good practitioner is found with his hand in our time is because the art of medicine is long, and it requires its practitioner first to be trained in the science of anatomy, which Gualia recounted, so that he knows the arrangement of the limbs and their forms, and their joining and separation; and has knowledge of the bones, the nerves and the muscles, and their number, and their exit points; and of the pulsating veins and the still veins, and the locations of their origin.
And therefore, Hippocrates says that with one name it is great, but in practice small, and especially in the art of the hand.
But we have already spoken of that in the introduction of this book; for he who is not knowledgeable in what we have said about anatomy, cannot avoid falling into error, by which men will die, as I have seen too many of those, for some are trained in this science and throw themselves into it without knowledge and without experience.
For I saw a foolish and inexperienced doctor who had incised a scrofulous abscess in the neck of a woman, and cut some arteries of the neck so that much blood flowed and the woman fell dead in his hands.
And I saw another doctor who attempted to remove a stone from a man who had advanced in age, and the stone was large, and he came to it and removed that stone with a piece of the bladder, for which that man died on the third day.
And I was already called to remove it, and I saw that because of the size of the stone and the condition of the patient, I recognized he would die from it.
And I saw another doctor who took a stipend from a duke of our land for surgery, and came upon a black eunuch who had a fracture in his thigh; and the doctor, with his ignorance, set the fracture over the wound with cushions and splints with tight bandaging, and did not leave any breathing room for the wound.
And after some days he loosened it according to his wishes, and rebandaged it, and left it for some days and commanded that the binding not be loosened; and so it remained until the thigh and foot were abscessed and reached destruction.
And I was called to him, and I prepared to loosen the binding, and he achieved calm and thus relieved his pains.
But however, the corruption was already established in the limb, so I could not stop it, and the corruption continued in the limb until the patient perished.
And I saw another doctor who pierced a cancerous abscess, and it was ulcerated for some days, until its malignancy was increased by its master (host).
The reason being that the cancerous ulcer is full of melancholic humor, thus it should not be completely removed by iron (surgery), but should be in a limb that can completely tolerate being removed.
And therefore, sons, it is necessary for you that the operation by hand be divided into two parts: one is the operation associated with health, and the other is work with which there is danger in the major conditions.
And I have already mentioned this in every part of this book where there is an operation in which there is error and fear; so it is necessary for you to observe this and leave that, so that the foolish do not find a way to speak and dishonor you.
And so take your tools with diligence and with the protection of God, and your patients with happiness and firmness.
And use the best way by the grace of God leading to health and praiseworthy success, and leave the terrible diseases of difficult healing, and put away your tools from what makes you fear, so that doubt in your faith and in your name does not evade you: for more remains for your glory, and higher in the world and ultimately for your sanctity.
Gualia has already said in some of his admonitions: "Do not treat bad diseases, so that you are not called bad doctors."
And I have already divided this book into three chapters.
The division of the book begins, and first of the first part. The first chapter arranged from head to foot, in the forms of the instruments and the tools of the cauteries, and what is necessary in the operation.
The division of the second part. The second chapter, on cutting and piercing, and on cupping, and on wounds, and on extracting arrows and similar things, all categorized and arranged by the forms of the instruments.
The third chapter, on restoration and dislocation, and on the healing of twists and similar things; a chapter arranged from head to foot, and on the forms of the instruments.
It is necessary for me to speak about the qualities of the benefits of that cauterization and its harms, and in which constitution cauterization is prohibited.
Thus I say that the discourse on the quality of the benefits of cauterization and its harms is a long and detailed discussion, subtle through science, and a hidden secret; and many wise men have spoken about it, and they have disagreed on it, and for me, a small portion of their discourses suffices, fearing prolongation.
Therefore I say that cauterization benefits any constitution universally which has or lacks matter, except for two constitutions, which are hot constitutions without matter.
In the hot and dry constitution with matter they disagreed; for some said that cauterization is beneficial for it, while others said the opposite, that cauterization is not suitable for an illness which is by heat and dryness, because the nature of fire is heat and dryness, and it is unsuitable for a hot and dry illness to be healed by something that is hot and dry.
And he who said the opposite of that said that by cauterization benefit is made in any hot and dry illness that comes in the bodies of men, because when you compare the body of man and its moistures to the constitution of fire, you find the body of man cold.
And I say through the discourse of that one, that I have already discovered this in some experiments.
However, it is not appropriate for someone who is not already experienced and practiced in the chapter of cauterization through thorough exercise to come upon this, and who has not known the diversity of the constitutions of men and the disposition of the diseases in themselves, and their causes, and their accidents, and the time span of those.
But in the other constitutions, there is no fear for you, especially in cold and moist illnesses, for all the doctors have agreed on those, and have not disagreed on the benefit of cauterization in those.
And know, sons, that the secret of healing by cauterization with fire and its goodness, as opposed to cauterization with burning medicine, is that fire is simple, whose action does not reach beyond the member which is cauterized and does not harm the other member adjacent to it except with little harm.
But the action of burning medicine reaches that in the members which are distant from it, and very quickly brings to the member a disease of difficult healing and very quickly seen.
But the fire, due to its sublimity and the goodness of its substance, does not do this except to remove excess. And it is already known to us by experiment and by long service of study in the art on the truth of the causes; and therefore I am excused from the length of the discourse. And if it were not that it is not read in my book, in this I would pause to you a hidden secret with fire, and the quality of its operation in bodies and of its expulsion by which it expels diseases, with a demonstrative philosophical discourse, which would be quickened in your minds.
And know, sons, that they disagree on the time in which it is suitable to do cauterization and they place the best time in spring.
And I say that cauterization is suitable at all times, because the harm from the accidental time is submerged in the benefit of that which is achieved with cauterization, and especially if the cauterization is for pains or necessary pains, strong and urgent, which do not suffer delay because it is feared that from those will come a malignancy, which is greater than the small harm entering from the time.
And this does not fall into your minds, oh sons, this is not thought by anyone except the common people and foolish doctors, that is, that the disease healed by cauterization does not recur all the time, and they place that as a result.
And the cause is not as they think because of this, because cauterization is only in place of medicine which alters the constitution and dries the moistures which are the cause for the pains to come.
But cauterization surpasses medicine by the swiftness of its power and virtue of its operation and the greatness of its lord.
And it is possible that the disease will return at some hour of the time, according to the constitution of the patient and the location of his disease, and the strength of it, and that which is prepared in his body from the accumulation of excesses in it, and that which has come to it in his acquisition of foods and similar causes; unless the disease in which cauterization is administered is a subtle disease in a member with few excesses and moistures, like the cauterization of a tooth for pain and similar to that, it is possible that in it that pain will not return, but this happens rarely.
And the common saying is that cauterization is the last remedy of medicine, and it is a true saying, not for what they understand in it, because they believe that it is not a cure that benefits with medicine nor with another cause except cauterization.
And the cause is contrary to that; and the intention that cauterization is the last remedy of medicine is not except when we administer the methods of healing in some diseases, and those medicines do not confer, and near the end of the cause one administers cauterization, and it benefits that. And then from here it falls that cauterization is the last remedy of medicine, not according to the intention which the common people and the many foolish doctors understand.
And the ancients said that cauterization with gold is better than cauterization with iron, and they did not say this except for the equality of gold and the sublimity of its substance.
And they said that the place of cauterization does not become rough, though this is not absolutely true, for I have already tested this and found that it does not do this except in some bodies and not in others, and cauterization with it is good and better than with iron as they said; but however, when you make cauterization with fire with gold, it is not clear to you, because it is ignited according to the quantity you want due to the toughness of the gold, and because it is prepared to receive cold; and if you apply ignition on it, the gold melts by the fire, and some layers fall into the fire, by which the artificer falls due to the occupation with it.
And therefore cauterization with iron is done by us more easily and more suitably to the correctness of the operation.
And I have already arranged this chapter on cauterization according to chapters, and I have arranged them from the head to the feet, so that I might alleviate the demand by giving what is needed.
The first chapter.
This cauterization helps with humidity and coldness on the brain, which are due to two causes, namely soda and the abundance of rheumatic flow called catarrh, from the head to the parts of the eyes and ears, excessive sleep, and pain of the teeth and throat, and generally any disease that comes from coldness, such as paralysis, sudden stroke, and similar diseases.
The method of this cauterization is this: firstly, you command the patient to evacuate the head with purgative medicine, cleaning it for three or four nights, according to the patient's strength, age, and habits.
And then command him to shave his head with a razor, and then make him sit between your hands on a square bench and place his hands on his chest.
Then place the edge of your palm near the well above the bridge of the nose between his eyes. However, when the middle of your finger locates the spot, mark that place with a probe, and then ignite the olive cautery iron, the form of which is:
And then place it on the marked spot with the probe, in such a way that it descends, with your hand gradually applying pressure, and you rotate it.
Then lightly lift your hand and check the spot, and if you see any part of the bone exposed, as much as a grain of wheat, then lift your hand, and if not, return with the iron or another iron, if it has cooled, until the bone is visible as I have told you.
Then take a little salt and dissolve it on the spot and leave it for three days.
Then place cotton soaked in vinegar over it and leave it until the scab from the burn is destroyed. Then treat the spot with tetraderm ointment until it is healed.
And some have said that as long as the wound remains open, it expels the humor better, making it more beneficial.
And some of them have said that the skin should be cauterized down to the bone, and the cautery should be held until some part of the bone's surface is burnt.
Then scrape off the burnt part of the bone and treat it.
Others have said that with the cautery, it should reach the marrow, pressing strongly on the bone until it falls, and this is the form of the minor alphecati: And they believe that from that spot the vapors of the head are expelled.
The wound should be kept open for a long time, and then be treated until it is consolidated.
But to me, these two kinds of cauterization have not been seen at all, except in some men as a means of precaution; and leave that, in my view, it is better for health, as it is; because the head is weakened by the breaking of its continuity as we have testified in other members, especially when the patient's head is of fragile nature.
And the first type of cauterization is safer and better in my view; use that one and work with that for health.
Second chapter.
Of the cauterization of the head.
When in the entire head there is chronic and long-lasting pain and this has been prolonged in the patient, and he uses warm packs, cupping, head purges, poultices, ointments, suppositories, chickens, and the like, and especially if it has already been cauterized with a cautery which we recount and none of those causes provide relief, then consider if the patient's head is of strong constitution in its nature; and it is not fragile and has found great coldness, then cauterize with another cautery a little below that. Then cauterize that on the horn of his head with a cautery until the skin is split. Incise the skin and expose the amount of bone which we have recounted and cauterize it with a cautery on the back part of the head in the place called "vas" — and it is that place where cupping is done. And ease your hand in that, and do not expose the bone, because the patient finds much greater pain from that than from any other head cautery.
And I will tell this cautery in its place, because the cautery with which the horns of the head and the occiput are cauterized is a more subtle cautery than that with which the middle of the head is cauterized; and this is its form.
Third chapter.
Of the cauterization of recent migraine.
When sudden pain arises in the part of the head, or pain is extended into the eye, evacuate the head with cleansing medicines, and administer the other treatment which I have mentioned in the divisions of diseases; and if all that does not help, then cauterization in that place is done in two ways: either with acute burning medicine or with iron.
But cauterization with burning medicine is that you take a clove of garlic, peel it, and cut off the ends of both heads. Then incise the spot of the pain in the temple with a broad lancet until a space is made beneath the skin, until it is hidden.
Then press on it with a stopper for gradual constriction, and leave it for three hours; then untie it and remove the garlic, and leave the wound for two or three days, then place cotton soaked in vinegar over it until the spot makes a scab.
Then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
And if you wish, do this with some burning medicines which I have written about in the twelfth treatise of burning medicines.
But cauterization with iron in this manner: ignite the cautery iron of which this is the form:
It is called "clavis clavis", because its head is like the shape of a nail in which there is some curvature, and in the middle of it there is a small eminence.
Then place it on the spot of the pain, and hold your hand — so that the artery beneath it is not burnt — and rotate the iron little by little with speed.
And if the amount that is burnt is approximately half the thickness of the skin, lift your hand so that the artery beneath is not burnt, so that no blood flow comes.
Then soak cotton in salt water and place it on the spot, and leave it for three days; then place cotton soaked in vinegar, and then treat with ointment until it is healed.
And if you wish, cauterize with the tip of the surgical knife according to the cautery, and beware of incision of the artery in this recent migraine.
Chapter Four:
On the Cauterization of Chronic Migraine
When you treat chronic migraine with the remedies we have previously described and those outlined in the sections on diseases, and if the treatment does not work, and you see that it is necessary to go beyond what was described in the initial cautery with medicine or cautery with fire, then you must ignite the cautery iron until it is white-hot. Mark the spot of the pain with a line about half the length of a finger, or nearly so; place your hand firmly once and press it until you cut through the skin and reach the bone.
However, it is necessary to avoid the continuation of the jaw, which moves during mastication, and to ensure that the muscle is not burned and the nerve that controls it is not damaged, to prevent spasms.
And it is crucial to be cautious about the blood flow from the artery that may be cut, as there is fear in cutting it, especially for those who are inexperienced and not diligent or trained; leave the operation at that initial stage.
I will come back with a reminder about the management of accidental bleeding from the artery in its proper place in this book.
And if you see that this disease is not addressed by this cautery and the patient's body is strong enough, cauterize in the middle of the head as we have described, and treat the wound until it is healed.
And if you wish, use the cautery method mentioned in the chapter on arterial constriction with a cautery with two blades; this is a better and more effective cauterization.
Chapter Five:
On the Cauterization of Ear Pain
When pain occurs in the ear due to cold and is treated with laxative measures and other treatments described in the section, and the pain persists, then ignite the cautery iron, which is called "puncta", of which this is the form:
Then mark close to the ignition, and move it around the entire ear as if revolving a wheel, or in the circuit of each segment if the pain is in each one. Extend yourself with the cautery from the root of the ear little by little after marking the spot with a probe.
If the cautery has ten points in the entire ear, or nearly so, treat the spots until they are healed.
Chapter Six:
On the Cauterization of Torticollis
Torticollis which is cured with cauterization is only of the types caused by phlegm, as we have said in the sections on diseases.
Avoid cauterization of the type that arises from dryness or from nerve spasm.
When you treat that type of torticollis with cups, head purges, and other treatments, and it does not improve, then you must cauterize the patient with three cauteries: one near the root of the ear, the second a little below the temple, and the third near the junction of the two lips.
And place your cautery on the opposite side of the affected part, because softening only occurs in the part that appears healthy.
The method of cauterization is to cauterize with the cautery iron at the right of the uppermost part of the ear, a little above the horn of the head, and at the temple on the other side, the length of which is about the length of a thumb, and apply your hand with the cautery iron until it burns about half the thickness of the skin.
This is the form of the cautery iron, and it is a type of surgical knife, the form of which is set out beforehand. However, it is more subtle when it is slightly like this; and the knife should be slightly thicker than usual.
Then treat the spot with the remedy mentioned earlier until it is healed.
Chapter Seven:
On the Cauterization of Chronic Apoplexy
When apoplexy is chronic and you treat it with the remedies we have mentioned and the medication does not help and the patient does not have a fever, then cauterize with four cauteries: on all the horns of the head; with a cautery iron in the middle of the head as we have described; and with a cautery on the nape of the neck. The method of cauterization is done as previously described.
And it is also cauterized on the pit of the stomach; and this is more posterior; then treat with what has been previously mentioned.
Chapter Eight:
On the Cauterization of Memory Loss Caused by Phlegm
At the beginning, the patient should be given large jars and pills that cleanse the brain.
Then shave the patient's entire head and place a cedar plaster on the nape of the neck, as described in the treatise on plasters.
Apply it several times, as it acts in a similar manner to cauterization. Do this according to the same order already described.
If the patient is healed by these methods, that is good; if not, then make three cauteries on the back of the head, descending from the uppermost part of the head to the lowest part of the neck. Place the cauteries about a finger's width apart, and treat them with the previously mentioned remedies.
But if you want to add more and the patient can tolerate it, then make a cautery in the middle of the head; and if you want to add even more, then cauterize over the two horns.
Then treat the area until it is healed; and let the cautery be olive-shaped as previously described.
Chapter Nine:
On the Cauterization of Paralysis and the Softening of the Entire Body
You should proceed with cleansing the head with jars and the remedies we have mentioned; then shave the patient's head, and cauterize with a cautery iron in the middle of the head, on all the horns of the head, on the nape of the neck, and with three cauteries on the vertebrae of the neck.
If necessary, in the case of softening of the body, if the disease is severe and the patient can tolerate it, then make four cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, and finally cauterize until the skin is deeply burned, and then lift your hand. Then treat the area until it is healed, and this should be an olive-shaped cautery.
Chapter Ten:
On the Cauterization of Epilepsy.
Epileptics are not to be cauterized unless the epilepsy is caused by phlegm.
Initially, you should cleanse their brain with cups and other treatments mentioned in the division if the patient is an adult and can tolerate the use of medicines.
If it is a child who cannot endure the medicines, administer fumigations and chewing substances that cleanse the brain for several days before the procedure, combined with suitable food.
Then shave the patient's head and cauterize the middle of the head following the previously described method, and another cautery on the back of the head, and on each horn of the head. If the patient is strong and can endure it, cauterize them as described for paralysis and softening of the body, on the cervical and dorsal vertebrae.
Use olive cautery as described before.
But if the patient is a child, use a subtler cautery.
Chapter Eleven:
On the Cauterization of Melancholia.
When the causes of melancholia are corrupted humors and thick phlegm, cauterize the patient with the same cauteries mentioned for paralysis.
If the cause of melancholia is an excess of black bile and the patient's body is moist, give them something to cleanse their brain as previously mentioned in the division.
Then shave the patient's head and apply a suitably made turban soaked in old sheep’s butter.
The patient should sit upright and be held from all sides.
Then apply liniment; old sheep’s butter is preferable. Heat it over the fire to a moderate temperature that the patient can tolerate. Pour this onto the middle of the head within the turban and let it cool.
Do this once a week with other suitable regimens until cured. Or, if preferred, cauterize the patient with small points in the same manner, ensuring you hold the cautery appropriately. This type of cautery moistens the brain evenly.
Then apply cotton soaked in butter or chicken fat.
Chapter Twelve:
On the Cauterization for Water Falling into the Eye.
When the onset of water in the eye is identified by the signs mentioned in the division, begin by giving the patient something to cleanse their head and avoid all humid substances.
Make them sweat in a steam bath for several days.
Then shave their head and cauterize the middle of the head. Additionally, cauterize the two temples if water is affecting both eyes, or one side if only one eye is affected.
Incise with the cautery all veins and arteries beneath the skin. The cauteries should be long and lateral on the temples.
Be careful to prevent bleeding when cauterizing; if bleeding occurs, stop it with any possible medicine.
I will provide further guidance on the extraction and excision of arteries and controlling bleeding.
Sometimes, cauterization is done on the neck below the two bones with two subsequent cauteries.
Chapter Thirteen:
On the Cauterization of Chronic Tearing.
When chronic tearing of the eyes is due to manifest veins and arteries on the exterior of the head, indicating cold phlegmatic excesses, cauterize the patient with the same cautery mentioned for the onset of water falling into the eyes, and a cautery in the middle of the head, and two cauteries on the neck below the two temples, and two cauteries on the neck below the two bones. If additional cautery is needed, cauterize on each side of the eye’s tail at the end of the eyebrow with a small cautery.
Chapter Fourteen:
On the Cauterization of Nasal Fetor.
When treatment for nasal fetor as previously mentioned in the division proves ineffective, prepare and administer purgative pills for three nights. Then shave the patient's head and cauterize with an olive-shaped cautery. Next, cauterize with a nail-shaped cautery on both eyebrows just below the hairline.
Be careful not to incise the artery.
The form of the nail-shaped cautery is as described.
Chapter Fifteen:
On the Cauterization of Softening of the Eyelids.
When the eyelid is softened by disease or humidity, cauterize the eyelid with a lunar-shaped cautery; and this is its form:
And if you wish, cauterize both eyebrows slightly with two cauteries in all parts, and they should extend from the temples. And the length of each cautery should be according to the size of the eyebrow.
Do not press too hard with the cautery, but just enough so that a third of the eyelid is affected.
Chapter Sixteen:
On the Cauterization of the Eyelid When Hairs Grow Inward and Hurt the Eye.
When the hairs of the eye grow inward and hurt the eye, cauterization can be done in two ways: either with fire or with a caustic medicine.
If using fire, instruct the patient beforehand to let their hairs grow long and straight; and if it hurts them at the root, bind their eyes with a bandage so that they are not hurt until the hairs have grown.
When the hairs have grown long and straight, place the patient's head on your knee, and mark the eyelid with a marking rod in the shape of a myrtle leaf, starting the mark near the hairs.
Then place cotton soaked in egg white or ptilli mucilage over the eye.
Then heat the cautery, which has this shape.
Then cauterize over the marked figure bit by bit, many times, until the surface of the skin, marked like a myrtle leaf, is properly burned.
A sign of the success of your work is when you see the eyelid is contracted and the hairs are elevated away from the eye.
Then lift your hand and leave it for three days, and then apply cotton soaked in butter until the scab is removed. Then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
But if the condition returns due to the hairs at their root and the eyelid is softened, cauterize it again as you did the first time.
If the hairs are on the lower eyelid, cauterize it until it is raised to its natural position and straightened so that the hairs do not hurt the eye.
The cauterization with caustic medicine involves instructing the patient to let the hairs grow long and straight, then preparing a piece of cloth in the shape of a myrtle leaf.
Then take known soap and an ounce of quicklime, mix them well, and do this quickly so it does not cool. Then spread the mixture on the cloth shaped like a myrtle leaf, and place it on one or both eyes; place cotton soaked in egg white under the eye, and position the patient's head on your knee.
Press the medicine slightly, but do not move it until the patient feels a biting sensation from the medicine, as if it were fire.
When the patient feels the biting sensation, remove the medicine and rub the area with your finger; when the sensation stops, remove the medicine and wash the eye with water; then see if the eyelid is already raised as it would be by fire or incision, and if not, apply the medicine again on the area where it was not effective, until the operation is complete and the eye is contracted.
Then apply cotton soaked in butter until the burn scab is removed, and treat it with ointment until healed.
You must be vigilant that no medicine falls into the eye during the operation, and if the eyelid softens again and the hairs cause pain, repeat the medicine on the softened area of the eyelid as you did initially, and treat it until it is healed.
This is the form of the myrtle leaf.
Know that human eyes vary in size, so your treatment should be adjusted accordingly.
The proper method will not be hidden from anyone who studies this art.
Chapter Seventeen:
On the Cauterization of a Fistula in the Lacrimal Duct of the Eye.
When treating a fistula according to the previous divisions of diseases and your treatment does not succeed, it is necessary to cauterize in the following manner: instruct the patient to rest their head on your knee, and have an assistant hold the patient's head steady so it does not move or shake.
Then place cotton soaked in egg white or ptilli mucilage over the patient's eye.
Next, heat the cautery,
which should be concave in shape, similar to a reed or vulture's feather, with one end suitable for the cautery.
If desired, it can be penetrating to the other end. And if you prefer, it can be a rod shape.
However, the concave one is better for your work.
Then press the fistula if it is open, extract the pus, and dry it. If it is not open, then open it and remove the pus.
Then place the well-heated cautery on the fistula, holding it from the eye to the nose to avoid errors or disturbing the patient, and apply the cautery to the tissue to cauterize it.
If the initial cauterization reaches the bone, that is good; if not, repeat the cauterization once or twice if necessary; then leave it for three days, and apply cotton soaked in butter and cover it with a drying ointment until healed.
But if after ten days it is not healed, then apply a strong corrosive medicine until the bone is exposed and treat it as described in the appropriate chapter.
Another method of cauterizing a fistula as suggested by some early practitioners is: locate the fistula and place a fine wick in it. Then pour a small amount (one ounce) of melted lead into the fissure; hold your hand steady with the wick to ensure the molten lead does not reach the patient's eye. Place cotton soaked in egg white or water over the patient's eye, as the lead will cauterize the fistula and heal it miraculously.
If the fistula heals with the cauterization and treatment mentioned, that is good; if not, it is necessary to create a nasal passage and redirect the fistula to the nasal cavity as described in its appropriate section.
Chapter Eighteen:
On the Cauterization of Fissures in the Lips.
Fissures often occur in the lips, especially in children's lips.
When you treat these fissures as described in the divisions and your treatment does not succeed, then heat a small knife-shaped cautery as follows:
The concavity of the cautery should match the fineness of a knife.
Carefully place the heated cautery in the fissures until it reaches the base of the fissures.
Then treat it with plaster until it heals.
Chapter Nineteen:
On the Cauterization of a Fistula in the Mouth.
When an abscess forms in the root of the uvula, palate, or tooth roots, and it breaks and becomes chronic, forming a fistula, and your treatment is ineffective, it is necessary to use a cautery. Heat the cautery to fit the fistula and insert it into the fistula, holding it until the hot iron reaches the base; repeat this once or twice if needed, and then treat it as previously described until it heals.
If the pus is removed and it heals, that is good; if not, it is necessary to expose the area and remove the decayed bone as described in the relevant chapter.
Chapter Twenty:
On the Cauterization of Teeth and Softening of the Gums.
When the gums are softened by moisture and the teeth are loose, and treatment with medicine does not help, rest the patient's head on your knee, and heat the cautery. The form of the cautery should be such that you place the cannula over the tooth and insert the heated cautery quickly, holding it until the patient feels the heat reach the root of the tooth.
Then remove your hand and repeat the cauterization a few times as needed.
Then the patient should fill their mouth with salt water, hold it for an hour, and then spit it out. This will firm the loose tooth, tighten the softened gums, and dry out the moisture.
Chapter Twenty-One:
On the Cauterization for Tooth Pain.
When tooth pain is caused by cold, frost, or worms, and treatment with medicine is ineffective, cauterization can be done in two ways: with cow's butter or with fire.
Cauterization with butter involves boiling cow's butter in an iron ladle, wrapping cotton around the end of a stick, dipping it in the boiled butter, and quickly placing it on the painful tooth until it cools.
Repeat this several times until the heat penetrates the root of the tooth.
If you prefer, soak wool or cotton in the butter, place it on the painful tooth, and apply a hot iron on top until the heat reaches the base of the tooth cavity.
However, cauterization with fire involves using a brass or iron cannula with some thickness to ensure the heat does not reach the patient's mouth.
Then heat the cautery, apply it to the tooth, and hold it until it cools; repeat this a few times as needed to relieve the pain, either the same day or the next day.
The patient should fill their mouth with good butter, hold it for an hour, and then spit it out. This is the form of the cautery.
Use whichever extremity you find most suitable for cauterization.
This is the form of the cannula:
Chapter Twenty-Two:
On the Cauterization of Extrophulos.
When extrophulos are caused by phlegm and cold humors and do not respond to digestive medicines, and you want to digest them quickly, heat the concave cautery, which should have two open ends for smoke to escape. Apply the heated cautery to the abscess once, or as needed, until it reaches the base of the abscess.
If the abscess is small, apply the cautery according to the size of the abscess, leave it for three days, and apply cotton soaked in butter until the burnt tissue is removed.
Then treat it with ointment, bandages, and cloths until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Three:
On the Cauterization for Hoarseness of Voice and Constriction of the Windpipe.
When moisture accumulates in the windpipe, especially when it is due to a cold constitution, the patient should initially be treated with laxative medicines. Then cauterize the area near the root of the throat at the depressed location.
Take care not to let the cautery reach the throat or burn the tissue excessively, but only a little.
Then cauterize the area at the junction of the neck near the last vertebra. The cautery should be nail-shaped as previously described.
Treat the area with the previously mentioned methods until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Four:
On the cauterization of lung disease and cough.
When cough and lung disease are due to cold humidity and the patient has no fever or tuberculosis and it is an old illness, then cauterize them with two cauteries on the furcula (collarbone) in the depressed places, lion-like and bulbous; and with another cautery in the middle of the chest between the two nipples; and let the cautery be a pointed type according to the previous form.
And if you wish, let your cauterization be point-like with the cautery called puncta, the form of which is described earlier in the section on ear pain.
And let there be points among them; and then treat them with the same method as before until healed.
And if the cautery has three branches for the cauterization: in one session, you can make three cauteries in this form:
Chapter Twenty-Five:
On the cauterization of the titillic, which is the head of the shoulder.
When the head of the shoulder is dislocated due to slippery humidity and it is not fixed at the time of its return to its usual position, and with a small movement it dislocates again, as we have observed, then you must first reduce the dislocation, and then the patient should be laid on their back or on their healthy side; and then raise the tendon that is in the inner part of the shoulder to the upper parts with the fingers of your left hand, if the joint is dislocated inward.
And then heat the cautery with two heads; and this is the form:
And then cauterize the tendon until it penetrates into the other side; and the figure of the cauterization will then have four heads of the cauteries.
And sometimes it is adjusted according to this calculation and therefore there are eight cauteries.
And then place cooked salt on the cautery, and then make the patient rest in tranquility, and the limb should not be moved for some time until it is strengthened.
And if the dislocation is in the upper parts and this is done later, then cauterize on the muscle with a pointed cautery or with many pointed cauteries, because the joint is confirmed and the humidity is removed and the patient is healed.
Chapter Twenty-Six:
On the cauterization of the stomach.
When cold and much humidity come to the stomach, causing it to deviate from its usual condition and this leads to a thickening of the tissues, and if you continue the usual treatments without success, then the patient should lie on their back, extending their arms and legs, and then cauterize with three cauteries: one below the chest the width of a finger with a pointed cautery, and two cauteries below on both sides of the first cautery, forming a triangular shape; and space them so they are not too close together to avoid causing putrefaction.
And the depth of the cautery should be two-thirds the thickness of the tendon.
And let the figure of the cautery be in this form without addition. If you prefer, cauterize with a large cautery in the middle of the stomach in this form; and let this be a circular cautery in this form:
And let the stomach be cauterized with pointed cautery for those afraid of this cautery: mark points on the stomach according to the amount needed, and then cauterize with the pointed cautery; and then treat it with the previously described method until healed.
Chapter Twenty-Seven:
On the cauterization of a cold liver.
When there is pain in the liver due to coldness or humidity or large amounts of gas, causing it to deviate from its normal state due to the expulsion of excesses, and the patient is not cured with the previously mentioned treatments, then the patient should lie on their back, and mark three cauteries in this form and this same amount on the liver below the hypochondria, where the human decubitus is.
And let the space between each cautery be the thickness of a finger, and let the cautery be along the length of the body straight.
And do not compress the area too much with the cautery.
And if the amount that is burned is the size of half the thickness of the tendon, and it should not be too tight; and if the patient is standing, then let them stand on their feet; if they are not standing, then they should lie down, with their legs extended and their arms raised.
And it is possible for you to cauterize these cauteries with a knife cautery if you have the skill and prudence in the art.
And be careful not to proceed too much in the cauterization, and let the whole thickness of the tendon be burned so that the entire body is burned and it reaches the intestines, because the tendon here is delicate; and know this.
Chapter Twenty-Eight:
On the perforation of a liver abscess with a cautery.
When an abscess forms in the liver and you want to know if the abscess is in the liver tissue or in its covering, if it is in the liver tissue, the patient feels heaviness and pain without sharpness.
And if it is in the covering of the liver, the pain is sharp and sometimes the doctors are already exhausted by treating it.
So the patient should be turned onto their back; then mark the place of the abscess with ink; and then heat the cautery which is shaped like a needle, the form of which was described on the previous page. And cauterize with that cautery until the entire tendon is burned, until it reaches the covering, so that pus may escape.
And then treat it as with other abscesses until it is healed.
And it is not suitable to use this form of cautery except by someone whose study in the art of medicine is advanced and who has treated many illnesses; this operation should only be attempted by experienced practitioners, but it is best to leave it to us.
Chapter Twenty-Nine:
On the cauterization of pleurisy.
The ancients said that the cautery made from the roots of aristologia for cold pleurisy is done in this way: take a long dry root of aristologia that is found and is the thickness of a finger.
Then submerge it in oil and heat it in the fire, and then cauterize with one cautery in the place between the continuation of the furcula (collarbone) and the neck; and with two small cauteries below the clavicles, slightly inclined to the area below the chin, and with two cauteries between the fifth and sixth ribs, slightly inclined to the back; and with another cautery in the middle of the chest; and another on the stomach, and three cauteries on the back; and one cautery in the place between the two shoulders, and two cauteries on both sides of the chest, below the cautery between the two shoulders.
And it is not necessary to press too much with the cautery, but it should be evident in the tendon during the procedure.
Some ancients also said that some people use a cautery of iron similar to a rod and heat it, placing it in the location between the ribs, until it reaches the abscess and pus is expelled, as we have said for the liver abscess.
But in this perforation with cautery, there is no fear, because one can die immediately or it can cause a fistula that cannot be healed.
Chapter Thirty:
On the cauterization of the spleen.
When you treat the disease of the spleen using the method we have mentioned in the previous section and your treatment does not work, then cauterize it in one of three ways, all of which are correct. One way is to make three or four linear cauterizations along the length of the spleen, similar to the cauterizations for the liver, as previously described.
There should be a space the width of a finger or a little more between each cauterization, and the manner of cauterization should be the same as described for the liver.
Do not press too hard with the cautery; the patient should lie on their back.
Another method of cauterization is to heat a cautery with two edges, as mentioned in the chapter on muscle dislocation, and lift the tendon located in the area of the spleen where the patient lies on their left side.
And your lifting should be along the length of the body, so that the cauterizations are made along the width of the body.
Then insert the two heated edges into the area to penetrate the tendon from the other side.
Then remove the cautery; now four cauterizations are made.
If you prefer, cauterize with a cautery that has three edges.
Then treat the cauterization wound until it stops discharging pus after a few days, as this is more effective than previous treatments.
Chapter Thirty-One:
On the cauterization of dropsy.
Cauterization is not normally used for dropsy except in specific cases.
When treating dropsy using the methods described previously, if your treatment does not work, then you should cauterize with four cauterizations around the navel, one on the stomach, one on the liver, one on the spleen, two between the vertebrae of the back, one on the right side of the chest, and one more on the stomach. The depth of the cauterization should be close to the thickness of the tendon.
Then leave the cauterized area open so that it discharges pus for a long time.
Do not stop the patient's treatment after cauterization until it is clear that the two intended purposes are achieved, which will hasten their recovery.
The shape of the cautery used for the abdomen should be like a nail, as previously described, and the one used for the back should be like an olive, as described before.
Chapter Thirty-Two:
On the cauterization of the feet and thighs.
When the feet are swollen with dropsy and filled with yellow water, you should cauterize the feet on the top between the little toe and the ring toe, lift your hand with the cautery without twisting it; then lift your hand and do not completely return it, because the yellow water will discharge.
The cauterization is done in this way: make two cauterizations on both thighs, and on each thigh, the cauterization should be done with the tip of the knife-shaped cautery. The cauterizations should be along the length of the thigh: one below the knee and the other in the middle of the thigh; and make two cauterizations on each thigh.
Leave the wound open without treatment for a long time so that some of the water can discharge from it.
Then treat it with the remedies we have mentioned.
Chapter Thirty-Three:
On the cauterization of the abdomen.
When the abdomen is affected by cold and humidity, weakening the digestive and retentive functions of the stomach and intestines, and it is treated using conventional methods without success, sometimes the patient may require cauterization for beneficial results. Then cauterize with a large cautery on the stomach as previously described for stomach cauterization, with a circular cautery, and four subtle cauterizations around the navel, using a thin nail-like cautery; and cauterize on the prominent part with one large cautery, or two cauterizations.
If the excess humidity persists and the patient can tolerate it, then cauterize the lower abdomen and intestines, adding two more cauterizations on the stomach and near the groin, as this treatment is highly effective.
Chapter Thirty-Four:
On the cauterization of old hemorrhoids.
When old hemorrhoids are present, or there are thick, cold humors or corrupted humidities, and they are treated as previously described without success, then cauterize with three cauterizations on the lower vertebrae of the small triangular back, and one cauterization above the navel, two finger-widths apart.
If you suspect that the patient's stomach is cold and the food is not digested, and their face appears swollen, then cauterize the stomach with a large cautery as previously described; also cauterize once on the liver, and once on the spleen with a nail-like cautery, leaving the cauterized area open for a while, and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter Thirty-Five:
On the cauterization of atheyl near their removal.
When you remove atheyl, heat the cautery that resembles a ray. Then insert the heated cautery into the removed atheyl, moving your hand until the cautery reaches the opening of the vein from which the blood flows.
Do this once or twice, because if there are many atheyl, then cauterize each one as described, and then treat them with the appropriate ointments until they are healed.
If you cauterize with a large cautery on the prominent part, it is more effective.
Chapter Thirty-Six:
On the cauterization of fistulas that form naturally in certain parts.
When nature does not respond to the treatment we have described, and the patient is frightened by it, they should be treated with cauterization.
Therefore, when a person has a fistula in a natural area, and it is old, oozing pus and corrupted humidities, the best approach is to probe it with a thin probe, and then measure the depth of it with the same probe.
Then heat the cautery that resembles a ray. Then insert the heated cautery into the fistula, according to the depth measured by the probe.
Repeat the cauterization on it until the corrupted tissues are burned away, once, twice, or three times, as needed.
Be careful not to burn nerves or veins; if the fistula reaches the bladder or intestines, avoid those areas; only perform this procedure when the fistula is in a fleshy area and you believe it is not deep.
Then treat the area until it is healed.
If the area is sealed and the material has been removed, and it remains this way for a long time, then know that it is completely healed.
If the materials are not removed, then know that it is deep or in the cavity of corrupted bone, or due to other causes that will be mentioned in their place.
Chapter Thirty-Seven:
On the cauterization of abscesses.
When abscesses cause pain due to cold or thick wind, and the patient's suffering is worsened, then you should cauterize the abscesses with a nail-like cautery, as previously mentioned.
Then quickly cauterize them a little below, resulting in three lined-up cauterizations, which will be more effective.
Chapter Thirty-Eight:
On the cauterization of the bladder.
When the bladder becomes weak and softened due to cold and humidity, causing the patient to be unable to hold their urine, then cauterize the lower part of the bladder, where the pubic hair starts, with one cauterization on the right side and another on the left side.
The length of each cauterization should be the size of the joints of the fingers; also cauterize the lowest part of the back, or with two cauterizations if necessary.
The cautery should be nail-like, as previously described.
Chapter Thirty-Nine:
On the cauterization of the uterus.
When cold and humidity affect the uterus, preventing conception and corrupting menstrual flow, causing delayed onset and painful menstruation, then cauterize the area with three cauterizations around the navel as described for the bladder; and also cauterize the lower back with one or two nail-like cauterizations.
Chapter Forty:
On the cauterization of hip dislocation.
When mucous-like humidity is deposited in the hip area, causing dislocation, and a sign of this is that one thigh is longer than the other when compared. And the location of the dislocation is found in the hollow area.
Then you should cauterize the patient on the hip area with a circular cauterization, after marking with a probe around the affected part so that the area falls in the middle of the circle.
The cautery should have the same form as described in the cauterization of the stomach.
If this cautery is not available, then cauterize with three large olive-like cauterizations.
The cauterization should be done to the depth of the entire scab; and then treat the area until it heals.
Chapter Forty-One:
On the cauterization of the sciatic vein.
When pain occurs in the hip area due to cold and humidity, and the patient is not cured by previous treatments, then the patient should be purged of thick humors with fetid pills or similar remedies; and then cauterize.
The method of cauterization can be of two types: cauterization with burning medicines or cauterization with fire.
There are two known methods, one of which involves cauterizing the hip area with three triangular cauterizations; and make them deep. The space between each cauterization should be the width of a finger, and the cauteries should be olive or large.
Sometimes the cauterization should be on the middle spot on the hip joint, resulting in four cauterizations.
If you prefer, cauterize with a circular cautery as described for hip dislocation, ensuring it burns through the entire scab.
This is the method for cauterizing sciatica when the pain is in the hip and not extending into the thigh.
But if the pain extends into the thigh, then cauterize with two cauterizations on the thigh where the pain is located; and with one cauterization on the ankle, four fingers from the outer side.
If the cautery is knife-like, the depth should match the thickness of the scab.
If the pain extends to the toes, then cauterize at the point of pain with three or four cauterizations, or more if necessary.
If the pain is below the knee towards the thigh, then cauterize there with one knife-like cautery.
Be careful in all cauterizations not to burn nerves or large arteries, as this can cause harm or contraction in the patient.
I have seen cases where cauterization on the ankle resulted in severe swelling extending to the foot, causing perforation and corruption of the entire foot. This often led to severe diarrhea and eventually death.
If the pain is in two places simultaneously, cauterize each area in the same manner.
Some experts describe a hip cautery shaped like a circular iron, half a palm in circumference, with an opening the size of a date stone or slightly smaller.
Inside this circle, there is another third; the space between them is the size of a walnut; these circles are open on both sides; their length is one or two nuts; these are well-made iron rings; this is their form:
Then, heat in the fire until it is red and sparkles, and then place it on the hip area, with the patient lying on their healthy side. Then cauterize with three round cauterizations at once.
Leave it for three days; then apply a poultice and leave the wound open for several days; and treat with ointments until healed.
Albucasis says: "Oh, sons, we rarely use this type of cauterization due to the fear of its effects and because few patients can withstand it."
But it is effective for those who endure it and locate the source of the pain.
Another type of cautery involves using two circles with perforated armules through which iron pins are inserted; their length should be two nuts or close to it.
The space between each should be the width of a finger; they should be open on the top and bottom, fitting together in this manner:
Place on the hip area, with the patient lying on the healthy side, and press your hand on the hip area; then pour hot water between the two circles, gradually heating it with a small fire, keeping it for an hour, and the patient should endure the biting and burning sensation — which feels like fire — until the biting stops.
Then remove your hand with the instrument and wash off the hot water from the entire hip area with fresh water.
Leave it for three days and apply a poultice until the burn's blackness disappears, leave it for a few days, and pus will drain; this is the final aid.
Then treat it with ointments until it heals.
If the pain extends to the thigh or leg, use the same method as for the hip.
This is the hot water method, which I have already described in the treatise on medicine correction. Prepare alkali and unslaked lime in equal parts, grind them and place them in a new pot with a small hole in the bottom, enough to fit a drop.
Place an enameled pot beneath the first, and pour fresh water over the alkali and lime to cover them by a finger's width, then press firmly with your hand, and let the water drip through the hole into the enameled pot below.
Collect this water, pour it over more fresh lime and alkali, making it very strong, useful in many medical operations and in cauterizing other body parts, as it acts like fire.
This is one of the medicines used for hip cauterization, such as vinegar and honey mixed with lime and soap.
Gualia recounts an ancient remedy and cure for hip and sciatic vein pain, highly praising it, saying it requires no other treatment, and that it heals in one application as he described, quickly entering the bath and emerging cured.
Take fresh rue, or if unavailable, fresh figs; crush them well with a bit of fat and place on the painful hip, thigh, or leg, and bind it.
Leave it for three hours or until the patient feels relief from the burning.
Then bathe the patient, and when the body is warm, expose it to the sun, as the pain will cease and healing will occur.
Be aware that cauterization with these medicines should only be used after body evacuation.
However, Escorides said that cauterizing the sciatic vein with goat fat is beneficial; do this by soaking wool in old oil, placing it between the foot's bones and the ankle.
Then take dry goat fat, burn it to charcoal, place it on the wool, and leave it until it is extinguished. Repeat this process until the biting sensation reaches the hip, alleviating the pain.
This method of cauterization is called cow cautery.
Chapter Forty-Two:
On the cauterization for back pain.
Sometimes pain is in the back due to various causes, such as a fall, an impact, excessive evacuation, and similar reasons, due to the infusion of cold and humid matter.
And cauterization is only applicable in this specific type caused by the infusion of cold matter.
Therefore, it is necessary, after evacuating the patient with purging pills or similar, to make three lines on the painful back area according to its width, marking with ink five or more cauteries on each line, depending on the patient's tolerance and the strength of the cautery.
The cauterization should be done with a pointed cautery.
And if you want, cauterize with three or four cauteries with a medium-sized cautery in this manner:
Chapter Forty-Three:
On the cauterization at the beginning of hump formation.
This disease often occurs in young children; the sign of its onset in children is a swelling and movement on their back.
You will find that the last vertebra of the back and its surrounding areas start to rise above the other vertebrae.
And when you see this and want to prevent it, then cauterize it with a circular cautery in this manner: That the cautery covers the entire area of the vertebra equally.
And if you want, cauterize around the vertebra with a pointed cautery in two or three lines. And the points should be close to each other.
Then treat the area until it heals with what we have mentioned.
But be careful not to use cauterization on humps caused by nerve spasms.
Chapter Forty-Four:
On the cauterization for gout and joint pain.
When joint pain is caused by cold humors that infiltrate each part of the body, and pain occurs in the feet, it is customarily called gout by doctors.
And so, when gout caused by cold is treated with a method of care and the pain is not removed, then cauterization is used.
And you cauterize it after evacuation around the joint of the foot with many cauteries.
And it should be olive-shaped cauterization, which is as follows:
And if it is necessary to make points on the surface of the foot, then do so with a pointed cautery. And if the pain moves to the knees or other joints, which often happens, then cauterize each knee with three or four cauteries, covering the entire area with this olive-shaped cautery.
And if many cauteries are necessary, then cauterize and do not press too hard with the cautery, just around the thickness of the skin.
And if the pain moves to the knee and back, then apply the cautery we have mentioned in its chapter.
And if the pain is only in the hands, called chiragra, then puncture around the joints, making two lines.
And if pain remains in the fingers, then puncture around the joints on each knuckle and on the thumb of the hand.
And if the pain moves to the elbows or muscles after a few days, then cauterize each part of the arms.
And do not evacuate the patient from a good regimen and the reception of medicines, for if a good regimen is followed and phlegm is evacuated with this cautery, they will be healed, God willing.
Chapter Forty-Five:
On the cauterization for ruptures.
When a rupture occurs in the groin and the intestines protrude before the hernia, and this is just beginning, it is necessary to tell the patient to stop eating on that day and use gentle substances to help the hernia descend from the abdomen.
Then place the patient on their back between your hands, and instruct them to hold their breath until the hernia or intestines appear.
Then return it with your fingers and mark with ink the rupture over the bone of the groin with a sign whose end resembles half a circle in the upper parts of the body.
Then heat the cautery, which is shaped like this:
Until it is very red and emits sparks. Then return the intestine or hernia to its cavity, and place your hand over the area to prevent the intestine from coming out.
You have already opened the space between the patient's thighs, placed a cushion under them, another servant on their legs, and another on their chest, holding their hands.
Then place the cautery on the mark with your hand, holding the cautery straight, and keep it there until you reach the bone of the groin, and repeat if you haven't reached the bone the first time.
And wisely observe the emergence of the intestine during the cauterization, so as not to burn the intestine and cause death or other serious harm to the patient.
And know that if you do not reach the bone with the cautery, your operation will not be effective.
And the cautery for children should be delicate according to their size and measurement.
Then treat the cautery site for three days with butter until the scar from the burn is removed.
Then treat it with other ointments until it heals.
And the patient should lie on their back for forty days until the wound and the place are sealed.
And it is necessary to adjust their nutrition according to the length of their healing time to support their abdomen, and to prevent the intestine from protruding during the recovery effort.
And when they want to get up after forty days, provide them with decent support, and keep them under observation for another forty days.
And they should refrain from labor, overeating, drinking, and loud voices, for if they follow this regimen of complete healing, they will be cured.
And I will describe the treatment for ruptures in other parts of the abdomen. If you want to prevent them from protruding at the beginning, cauterize the rupture with a round cautery according to its size, ensuring that about a third of the skin is removed. Then treat it with what we have mentioned, and it will not be retracted.
Some earlier practitioners have said that a rupture should be cauterized with a triangular cautery in this shape, as it is marked with a probe:
A line is placed horizontally on the upper side of the rupture, and another line above; then place your hand with a nail-like cautery, but the first cautery is lighter and better.
Chapter Forty-Six:
On the cauterization for twists.
When pain occurs in some members due to a fall or an impact, and that pain persists for a long time and does not respond to medication, it is necessary to cauterize with a pointed cautery.
And the cautery should be done according to the size and strength of the member, and the location of the twist and pain.
Therefore, if it heals with the first cautery, it is good; if not, then repeat the cautery. For the custom of these pains is that they are punctured from one member to the next, and it is necessary to continue with cauterization until the patient is healed.
Chapter Forty-Seven:
On the cauterization for leprosy.
Leprosy sometimes benefits greatly from cauterization, especially if the type of leprosy is caused by the putrefaction of phlegm and melancholy.
And so, when you want to cauterize it, consider if the leprosy is starting and you have treated it with what we have mentioned, and if it is not stopping, and you fear the disease will spread throughout the patient's body, then cauterize the head with five cauteries: one in the middle of the head, a second lower down, near the forehead at the hairline, two on the horns, and one at the back, on the nape or neck.
And press your hand with the cautery slightly until it imprints a small mark on the bone, and the scabs are removed, so that the release of thick vapors is facilitated; and cauterize the spleen as previously described.
And if the leprosy has already spread over the patient and is visibly manifest, then you must cauterize it with those cauteries we have mentioned on the head; and with a cautery on the tip of the nose, two cauteries on the cheeks, two cauteries on two vertebrae of the neck, six cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, a large one near the tailbone, one on the elbow, two on the hips, one on each hip, two on the knees, one on each knee, two on the elbows, two on the muscles, two on the soft parts of the chest; and cauterize it on all the joints of the fingers and toes, on each heel, and on the joints of the hands.
And be careful not to burn the nerves over the heel.
And sometimes it is cauterized with a cautery on the bone of the groin, another on the mouth of the stomach, and another on the liver.
And know that the more cauteries you apply, the more beneficial and effective it is; and know that the patient does not feel pain in the cautery as healthy people do, because their body is already numb.
And the cauteries should be large or small depending on the members and joints as previously described for the types of instruments.
Then treat the cautery with cooked barley flour with honey and other treatments until it heals.
Chapter Forty-Eight:
On the cauterization for stupor.
When any limb is numb or asleep and has been treated with medicines, oils, and plasters but is not healed, then you must cauterize the numb limb with cauteries according to its size and importance.
And let your cautery penetrate the thickness of the skin a little. Then treat it with ointment until it heals.
Sometimes numbness that affects the hands and feet, the vertebrae of the back, and the emergence of the nerves that move the limb is cauterized, and the numbness is removed.
But only someone knowledgeable in the anatomy of the limbs and the emergence of the nerves moving the whole body should perform this.
Chapter Forty-Nine:
On the cauterization of albaras (white patches), which is morphea.
When albaras is old and does not respond to any medical ointment, then cauterize it with a deep cautery slightly, according to the thickness of the skin, until the whiteness and color are removed and the color is changed.
Then treat it with barley and lentil flour mixed with rose oil, leaves of arnagrosa, and blood of a pigeon or swallow. Each should be in equal parts, all mixed together, and then spread on a cloth and placed on the area until it heals.
Chapter Fifty:
On the cauterization for cancer.
When cancer is beginning and you want to remove it, then cauterize it with a circular cautery around its perimeter.
Wise men say it should be cauterized with a final cautery in the center, but I have not seen this done, as I fear it might ulcerate, and I have seen this happen sometimes.
It is better to cauterize around it in a circle as we have said, or with multiple cauteries.
Chapter Fifty-One:
On the cauterization of abscesses.
When any abscess is slow to mature, either because of excess causing it, the patient's old age, their decrepit state, low blood, or the weather, and you want to hasten its maturation, then cauterize around it with many small puncturing cauteries; then leave it as it will hasten the maturation.
But if you want to pierce it with a cautery, then heat the cautery in this shape: place it in the middle of the abscess until it penetrates the skin.
And if the cautery is in that lower area, so the body of the abscess is relieved; then treat it until it heals.
Chapter Fifty-Two:
On the cauterization of corrodent ulcers.
Corrodent is a moving corruption in the limb, which eats it away like fire consuming dry wood.
So if you see this corrodent in a place that can tolerate fire cautery, then heat several small nail-like cauteries, making them large as needed for the location of the corrodent.
Then cauterize all around it until you eradicate all the corrosion and no trace of it remains.
Then leave it for three days, and apply a mixture of ground alum and oil on the cauterized areas until all the scabs are removed and all the corruption is gone.
Then treat it with ointments that promote flesh growth.
But if after three days you see that the flesh is growing healthily without corruption, it is good; otherwise, repeat the cautery on what remains in the corrupted places.
Corrodent is sometimes treated with sharp medicine, which is applied in place of cautery. However, fire cautery is usually more effective.
I have already discussed the treatment with sharp medicine in another section, so use that when necessary.
Chapter Fifty-Three:
On the cauterization of calluses and similar conditions.
Often, a disease called "clau" (callus) appears on the lower parts of the foot, causing thick, painful skin that injures the foot; cautery for these can be done in two ways: with fire or with hot water.
Fire cautery involves heating a concave cautery tool resembling a vulture's claw, made of iron, matching the size of the callus, with thin edges.
Then place the heated tool on the callus and move it around the callus until the cautery penetrates the callus completely. Leave it for three days until it starts to throw off the callus. Then plaster it with crushed wild mallows mixed with salt, and leave the plaster on overnight, as it will eradicate the callus from its roots.
Then treat the area with a flesh-generating ointment until it heals.
If this doesn't work, and this often happens on the skin surface, especially on the hands and feet, then you need to apply hot water cautery. Take a cannula made of brass, iron, or a vulture's feather, and place it on the callus; pour a small amount of hot water into the cannula, compressing it fully with your hand, and pressing the cannula's edge into the callus root. The hot water will find its way to the root. The patient should endure the hot water's bite for an hour, then remove it, as the callus with its roots will be eradicated.
Do the same for all such calluses on the body.
Then treat them with ointments that generate flesh once the callus roots are eradicated.
Chapter Fifty-Four:
On the cauterization for tremors.
When someone has tremors due to cold in the nerves, or from a fever caused by cold, or other similar causes, then cauterize them with four or five cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, placing one cautery between each vertebra; and one on the chest, and one on the stomach with an olive-shaped cautery.
This will calm the tremor and hasten the healing of the cold-induced illness.
Chapter Fifty-Five:
On the cauterization of white pustules affecting the body.
When the body is afflicted with white pustules, which are caused by thick, corrupt materials; at the onset, cauterize each pustule with a thin cautery stick made from myrtle wood, its tip burned; or with long erestology roots; or with a lenticular cautery.
Sometimes, this cautery is used at the beginning of their expulsion in this manner; they are not burned through, and the excess causing them is disrupted, thus they heal.
However, this should be done after bloodletting.
Chapter Fifty-Six:
On the cauterization for arterial bleeding caused by an incision.
Often, arterial bleeding occurs due to an incision from an external wound, from the incision of abscesses, or from cautery of a limb, and such cases are difficult to manage.
When this happens, quickly apply your hand to the artery's opening, pressing your index finger firmly over it until the blood flow stops.
Then place several small and large olive-shaped cauteries in the fire, blowing on them until they are very hot.
Take a small or large cautery, depending on the location and wound size, and quickly apply it to the artery as you remove your finger, keeping it in place until the blood flow is cut off and stopped.
If blood is expelled from the artery's opening when you remove your finger and apply the cautery, quickly take another cautery prepared in the fire and continue this until the blood is restrained.
Be careful not to burn the nerve that is there, to avoid causing further harm to the patient.
And know that when blood flows from an artery, it is not possible to stop the bleeding, especially if the artery is large, except by one of four methods: either with a cautery as we have mentioned, or by extraction when it is not drawn out, for when it is drawn out, the ends are better burned and the bleeding is stopped, or it is tied with a thread or by strong binding, or medicines with the property of stopping bleeding are applied to it; and they are pressed with an appropriate compress.
But one who administers proper restriction by binding and compressing with cloths or by applying burning substances and similar methods, will not succeed except late.
But if this happens to someone and neither the doctor nor medicine is present, then act by calmly placing the index finger over the opening of that vein as we have said, and apply strong pressure until the blood is stopped from the upper part of the wound, and the finger is not removed from the artery; and the artery is sprinkled with very cold water until the blood is coagulated and thickened; and then consider whether it needs cautery or medicine.
On incisions and punctures and cupping and phlebotomy and wounds and their like.
We have already discussed in the first chapter all diseases for which cautery with fire is suitable, and with burning medicine, and the occasions and causes for it, and the necessary instruments with the forms of the cauteries; and I have arranged the chapters from the head to the feet.
And I will proceed in this chapter in the same manner, so that it may be easier for those seeking it, to find what they have requested.
And before I begin, it is necessary, sons, that you learn that in this chapter, there is something terrifying that is also in the first chapter of cauterization.
And for that reason, great caution is necessary, because in the operations described in this chapter, many times evacuation involves blood, in which life is contained, through the spilling from a vein, incision on an abscess, opening of a fistula, cleaning a wound, extraction of an arrow, or cutting of a stone, and similar procedures. And all these are accompanied by fear and trembling, and in most of them, death can occur.
And I warn you not to let hesitation fall upon you, because sometimes you may encounter men with many different kinds of diseases. And some of them will become angry with their illness and will be on the verge of death, due to the severity of what their illness entails, and due to the prolonged duration of their illness, and with the illness, there is fear which signifies death.
And some of them will give you their possessions and say that they hope for a cure, while their illness is pernicious.
And thus, it is not proper for you to agree to help those who come to you in this manner; and let your caution be greater than greed and rapacity; and do not undertake any case unless you have certain knowledge that verifies it for you, and which brings praiseworthy success.
And in the treatment of all your patients, use prognosis and indication of that which turns into health, for this helps you in acquiring fame and memory, praise, and glory.
Oh, sons, may God inspire you with His understanding and not take away from you direction and protection, for that is in His hands.
And I have ordered this chapter, distinguishing by chapters as in the chapter on cauteries, from the care of the head to the feet, so that it is easier for you to find what you seek and what you need.
First chapter.
On the treatment of water which is contained in the heads of children.
Know that this illness often occurs in children during childbirth or when the nurse squeezes the child's head too tightly.
And sometimes it happens for an unknown hidden reason.
But I have only seen this illness in children; and in all those I saw, death followed quickly, and so it seemed to me a neglected task in them.
And I have seen a child whose head was filled with water, and his head was so enlarged that he could not hold it up due to the size of his head, and the humidity increased until he died.
And this moisture is either collected between the scalp and the bone, or it is collected under the bone above the brain.
For the operation in that case, if the moisture is between the scalp and the bone and the swelling is small, then it is necessary to cut in the middle of the head with a fissure in the width, and the length of the fissure should be about two nuts so that the moisture can flow, and this is the shape of the incision:
And if the moisture is more increased and the swelling is larger, then there are two fissures in this manner:
And if the moisture is under the bone, a sign of it is that you see the seams of the head open in all parts and the water is squeezed out when you press it with your hand at the inner parts, and this is not hidden from you. It is necessary to cut in the middle of the head with three fissures in this manner:
and near the incision, extract all the moisture; and then tighten the fissures with cloths and sponges; and then anoint the area with wine and oil until the fifth day, and then loosen the bandage and treat the wound with tentacles and ointments, and bind the head with equal pressure; and feed the patient with dry food with little moisture until the part is strengthened and healed.
And there is another manner of incision, which considers where the swelling and collection of water appear, for sometimes it is more in the back, or in the front, or on the right, or on the left; and then attend to the incision where the swelling and collection of water appear, and do this as best as possible.
And observe not to cut the artery, lest there be a flow of blood and the patient die from that flow of blood with the evacuation of moisture.
Second chapter.
