And if you feel the probe on your finger already exposed penetrating by itself, other than what you feel between your finger and it being tissue or flesh, then you know certainly that it is penetrating; and then do not work on it, because there is no cure for it, as we have said.
But some might say that it heals in some men late.
And of the treatment by which help is expected is that you use a slender cautery according to the width of the fistula, as described earlier, and insert that heated cautery into the fistula until it reaches around the anus; and then repeat that two or three times until you know that all those fistulous tissues that are like the shaft of a bird's feather are burned.
And then treat it with bandages soaked in vinegar until all those burnt tissues fall off, and then treat it with medicines that generate and consolidate flesh, and if it is healed, it is good; and if not, then it is not healed at any time by any other treatment.
And if the probe enters, and does not penetrate into your finger which is in the anus, and between it and the probe there is a thick layer of flesh or tissue, and sometimes the fistula is at the surface of the skin, then cut the skin from the beginning of the fistula, and you cut with the probe, and be in the fistula until the cut reaches the place where the end of the probe reaches, and by the probe, it is freed and falls.
And then cleanse those fistulous tissues which are like the shaft of a bird's feather and ensure no cause remains from them.
But if a flow of blood comes between you and your operation when you cut those tissues, then the best thing and the lightest instrument is to cauterize with fire or sharp medicine, because cautery with fire has two good effects: it burns those adjusted tissues, and stops the blood and dries the moisture.
And then treat it with bandages soaked in vinegar or in crushed things until the place produces scabs, and then treat the wound with ointments that generate and consolidate hard flesh.
And these are medicines that have astringency and drying; when it is restored, it is already healed and do not fear recurrence.
But if the fistula already reaches into the anus and is elongated from the surface of the body, then insert your finger into the anus and examine with it.
And if you then feel the probe and between you and it there is a layer of tissue or flesh, and it is near penetration, then there is no operation in it except by a method of trust and hope.
The treatment involves one of three methods: either cauterize it as we have said; or cut it until you reach the cavity of it so that it is possible for you to insert the necessary tools and the proper treatment; and do not proceed much with cutting so that you do not cut the muscle containing the anus, and cause the patient to have involuntary discharge; and then treat it as we have said because it will heal very quickly; or extend that layer and cleanse that pus by cutting it near the anus, as we have said, with a probe or another sharp instrument, and then cleanse those fistulous tissues which are in the cut until you can remove them; and then try to restore the entire wound with cutting, and if the upper opening is not consolidated, and the fistula remains open in the part of the anus, it will be easier for the patient. And sometimes the fistula is ended in this way: it is when you insert the probe into the fistula, and it is on the side of the anus around the surface of the body with the skin and at the edge of the anus, then take a probe with a perforated tip, which is like a needle for sewing leather according to this form:
and insert into it a thread wrapped with five threads or around those; and then insert the probe with the thread into the fistula until you reach the cavity of it.
But if it is penetrating into the edge of the anus near inside, then you extract the thread from that hole so that you insert your finger into the anus; and pull out the end of the thread and join the two ends together, and tighten each, and leave it for a day or two, and then when you cut more into the flesh, tighten it well until those tissues that are between the two ends of the thread are cut and fall.
And then treat the wound until it is consolidated and healed.
But if the opening of the fistula is not penetrating, then make it penetrate, in whatever way is prepared for you, except that it is much in the anus, for you must not do it so that you do not cut the muscle; and then treat it until it is healed.
The form of a spatum spinos with which the fistula is cut: its curve is very sharp and the other part is not sharp, so that you do not cut what is not necessary, as sometimes.
On the suturing of hemorrhoids in which blood flows in the incision of them.
Hemorrhoids are in two ways, for they are either between the anus looking like coals, or as if they are like clusters of grapes. And of those some are small and some large, and blood flows from them continuously and some are single, and there are many, and they are outside the anus, and at its edges.
But those that are outside the anus are mostly due to little moisture, from which flows yellowish water or a little blood with continuous discomfort, and according to the color of the body.
And the treatment of those that are inside the anus is that you instruct the patient to sit and strain until the anus protrudes and the hemorrhoids appear to you. Then you quickly suspend them with hooks or hold them with your nail, and then cut them towards their roots.
But if the hooks do not hold them because of their moisture and softness, then take them with a rough cloth or cotton or wool blanket, and pull them out with your fingers, and then cut them and sprinkle on them some hot powders near the incision, so that they act on them in place of cautery; or cauterize them as previously mentioned in the chapter on cauterizing. And then treat them with the other treatment until they are healed.
And if the anus does not respond to evacuation, then use a small biting enema on the patient, and wash with it what is in the anus so that it responds to evacuation lightly when the patient strains.
But if the hemorrhoids are outside the anus, then the treatment of them is easier, and it is that you take them with your nail, or take them with a hook, and cut them, and then treat them as we have said until they are healed.
And for the one who dislikes incision, you should administer suturing of them in this manner: take a thread wrapped and insert it into a needle and then extract the hemorrhoids to the upper parts, and make it penetrate with the needle at the root of the other part, and wrap the two ends of the thread in the lower part of the needle, and this transversely; and tighten the hemorrhoid with a strong tie, and then knot the thread and extract the needle.
And do this to all, and leave one of them and do not sew it, so that the excess blood runs from it.
And then place over the anus a cloth soaked in rose oil or plaster; and place it over the anus and instruct the patient to rest. And then leave it until it falls; and when it has fallen, treat it with ointments or with other treatment until it is healed.
And when they are old and do not heal with that medicine, then you must scrape them with the tip of a spatula or with your nail until they are made moist and remove the uppermost scab, which prevents them from healing; and then treat them until they are healed.
Then you remove them more vigorously than the first time until much blood flows and they are excoriated; and then treat them until they are healed.
Know that "alamermar" is a corn, and it is nothing but a round knot resembling the color of the body and similar to the head of a nail, and it is formed on the whole body but mostly on the parts below the feet and the toes, and it causes pain in those areas.
Then you must incise around the corn, hold it with scissors or a hook, and remove it from its root; and then treat it.
And if you want, cauterize it as described in its chapter.
But a wart is a small, rough, round prominence on the surface of the body, and often appears on children's hands.
And its treatment is to stretch and incise it, or to bind it with thread or skin until it falls off. And if you want, cauterize it, as we have said, with fire or with a sharp medicine.
But "formica" is a small, felt-like or large fistulated prominence on the surface of the body, moving a lot in the flesh, and when it is touched, it feels like an ant bite, and it is formed around the body.
And its treatment is to take a tube made of goose feather or vulture feather, and make it strong, and place it over the "formica" so that it contains the circle of the tube around it; then rotate your hand with the tube until you incise around the "formica," and place it deep in the flesh, and eradicate the "formica" from its root.
And if you want, do this with a brass or iron tube in this way:
The upper part of the tube should be narrowed, closed (that is, not perforated), wrapped so that the handling of it with the finger is facilitated and its rotation is smooth.
And if you want, cauterize the "formica" as previously described in the chapter on cauterization of atheyl.
On the form of the instruments used to administer enemas.
Sometimes enemas are made of silver or brass or cast or hammered bronze; and sometimes these instruments are made small and large according to those to whom they are administered, because those used for treating small children should be small; and for those whose anuses are delicate or painful, their enemas should be small.
The form of a large and small enema:
The top of the tube to which the bag is attached should be wide in this manner; and it should be at the place where the bag is tied, as usual.
And the lower end, which is inserted into the anus, should be closed (that is, not open) and very smooth, narrowing to a fine point; and on one side there should be two holes, and on the other side one hole, and the size of the hole should be according to the thickness of the roots and a little thicker.
And the bag in which the medicines are placed should be made of animal bladder or sheep skin, and its root should be a palm in length.
And it should be perforated with many holes as it is wrapped; and the space between the holes should be the thickness of a finger.
Then insert into those holes strong bile of raw bile, which should be from ten gallbladders, or around them; and attach the bag to it; and when the bag is placed on it, it should be tightened at the top of the enema tube with strong constriction between two markings, and then administer the enema through each hole: But the quality of administering the enema with it is that you fill the bag with the medicine, which should be heated according to the patient's tolerance; and be careful that it is neither too hot nor too cold.
Then the patient should lie on their back, raise their legs, and place a pillow or sheet under them; then anoint the end of the enema tube with oil or egg white or fenugreek mucilage or something similar; then insert it into the anus easily, and the administrator should take the bag and squeeze it with both hands with force until everything in the enema tube is evacuated into the intestine; then remove the enema tube and the patient should lift their legs against the wall.
And if the medicine forces a bowel movement, the patient should hold it in as long as possible; and if they sleep with it for the whole night, it is even better for the treatment.
But if you administer the enema in cases of diarrhea and its exit causes burning, then you should repeat the enema until it is healed.
And if there are hemorrhoids or painful abscesses in the anus, then the enema tube should be very smooth and narrow, like the enemas used for children.
On the treatment of wounds.
I have already mentioned in the section on the treatment of wounds what is suitable for the book, and now I will describe the treatment of those with manual techniques and medicines according to the procedure.
So I say that the wounds are varied according to what causes them and according to the location on the body where the wounds occur.
And the causes for which many times wounds are made are such as the throwing of stones, or incision by sword or knife, or striking with a spear, or with sticks, or with arrows, or similar to those from many causes.
But the wounds that are on the parts of the body are such as a wound that falls upon the head, or the neck, or the chest, or the abdomen, or the liver, and similar to those of the limbs.
And I am recounting the treatment of some wounds so that you apply that reasoning and rule to other wounds.
And I will start with the simple wounds of the head, for the consideration of the complex ones will come at the beginning of the third chapter.
So I say that when a simple wound occurs on the head and it is not a fracture, then consider: if it is from the throwing of a stone or something similar, and it has only cracked the skin and is a large wound, and you fear for the patient the occurrence of a hot abscess, then promptly perform phlebotomy on the cephalic vein, and do not delay; and extract from the patient the blood according to the strength of the person unless some cause prohibits phlebotomy, especially if the blood that comes out of the wound is little.
And let the phlebotomy be on the opposite side of the wounded part; and the patient should avoid repletion, wine, and heavy foods.
But if some cause prohibits bloodletting, then let the patient reduce their food intake and avoid meat and wine.
And place on the wound, if a hot abscess occurs, cotton soaked in rose oil or in wine in which there is astringent.
But if you are sure of a hot abscess, then place on the wound, if it is recent with its blood and the air has not altered it, the powder which I will mention shortly.
And if the air has already altered it, then place some ointments on it until it is healed.
But if the wound is large or is by incision of a sword and the like, and the lips of it are not joined by powders, then join them with sutures as I will describe for their suturing.
But if the wound has already exposed the bone and tendon, and there is no other retention but a small suspension, then incise it and treat the wound with medicines and restore the flesh next to the skin, and instruct the patient to abstain from foods that are heavy: heads and cooked forms and similar to them, until it is healed.
And if you find putrefaction in the skin and it does not adhere to the bone, then you incise all that is rotten and remove it, and then treat it.
But if in these wounds there is an artery or vein from which blood can flow, and it is not stopped by medicines, then inquire about the artery, and if you find it not incised, then you incise it with a spatula, or tie it; and if necessity forces you that what we have said does not suffice, then cauterize until the blood is stopped.
And if these wounds are simple and small, then their cause is minor; so you sprinkle on them this powder, so that they are healed before the air changes them.
And the method of the powder is this: take one part oil of mastic, two parts dragon's blood, three parts unslaked lime; let all be very finely ground and sifted, and let the wound be filled with it, and be compressed with good compression until it adheres well to it, and be coagulated with the blood.
And then compress it on top with powders and leave it, and do not untie it while it is thus until it is not abscessed, for the medicine is not removed from it until it is healed.
And sometimes do this only cautiously, when you do not have mastic and dragon's blood present.
And always do this same operation on large wounds when it is necessary that it be placed on them and they be compressed, and these are recent wounds with their blood, which the air has not altered.
And if the air has altered them, especially when it is in summer, then it is not appropriate to apply this powder, but instead treat them with what we have mentioned, so that you place on them some digestible ointments, or you place on them powders of barley flour with water and honey until they remove the rottenness; and then treat them with the other treatment until they are healed.
But if with the wound a fracture of the bone occurs, and it is small, then extract it with gesture.
And I will tell the cure of fractures of the head in what is to come.
From the wounds of the neck.
But if the wound occurs on the neck, then there is no difference in treatment compared to a simple wound on the head.
But if the nerve or artery in the neck is cut, then if it is a nerve, do not use astringent agents like zinc and alum, as they are harmful to the nerves and can cause contraction. Do not apply any cold substance to the wound, as the nature of the nerves is cold, and they are connected to the brain, which is the highest of all organs.
And treat the wound with light medicines, such as washing it with fresh water, sometimes infused with rose oil or not, and then washing it again with fresh water and applying moist ointments.
But if the wound is large, then administer sutures, or close its edges, and treat it with powders until it is healed.
But if the wound is deep or occurs in a decaying area, then perforate it at the lowest part.
But if an artery is cut in the wound and there is a flow of blood, then separate or dry it, or tie it, or cauterize it if necessary.
But if the vertebrae of the throat or neck are cut and the veins of the neck are saved, then align the edges of the wound with sutures over the throat and do not touch it; first, align and reduce it to its natural shape, then secure it properly, and leave it for a few days; then treat it with ointments that have astringent and stimulating properties until it heals.
But if the wound in the bone has caused damage, then treat it, and if there are bone fragments, remove all that you can; and leave what you cannot remove until the wound decays, and it can be separated and the removal is easier.
And if the wound is recent, then apply the recommended powder to it. But if a fistula has formed in the wound, then treat it with what is described in its chapter.
If it is a strike from a spear or knife, and you see its depth, then consider if air comes out of the wound when the patient breathes; then know that the wound is mortal.
And if it is not deep and the wound is recent with its blood, then apply the powder from the first hour, and do not compress it so that the blood is not retained in its depth and returns to the heart and kills the patient, but apply an attractive ointment and loosen it twice a day.
And if the ointment is not available, then place old cotton in the wound's opening to absorb the moisture; and make the patient sleep on the wound so that what is inside runs out.
But if three days or more have passed and the patient has not experienced spasms or bad heart tremors or breathing difficulties, and other good conditions are observed, then know that the wound is safe; and then treat it with poultices and other treatments until it heals.
But if its healing is delayed and it remains open all the time, then know that a fistula has formed; treat it according to its chapter.
And if the wound is from an incision by a sword or knife and is simple on the surface of the chest or back, then treat it with what is mentioned for sutures if it is large, or with powder if it is small.
But if damage to the bone has occurred and pieces are cut off, then twist the wound and quickly remove those pieces if they are separated. But if they are not separated, then leave them until the wound decays, making their extraction easier.
And for other wounds occurring in other limbs, the judgment in treatment is the same as what we have said.
From the wounds of the abdomen.
The condition that occurs in the abdomen can be large, small, or moderate.
Small disruptions are difficult to manage in another way, and so their reduction is more difficult if you do not promptly return them to their place, as they can swell and thicken, making repositioning difficult.
Thus, a moderate disruption is best, as it is not difficult to reduce the intestine, unlike in the other two types mentioned before.
Know that the suturing of the abdomen follows four methods, but two sutures are common and suitable for other body wounds.
And two other sutures are specific to abdominal wounds, but they can also apply to other wounds.
And I will explain each one to you in detail.
So I say that when the wound is small and something from the intestine protrudes and is difficult to reduce, it is due to either the smallness of the disruption, as we have said, or when the intestine has become swollen from the cold air.
And when this happens, you must warm it by soaking a sponge or cloth in warm water alone or boiling it with skink, cypress, and spikenard, and apply it to the intestine until the swelling subsides.
Sometimes, use a solution with astringent properties, which is better than plain water for reducing the swelling.
And when you see the swelling has subsided, moisten the intestine with water in which marshmallow and mallow have been boiled, as this eases its repositioning with little effort.
But if repositioning is still difficult with this treatment, then slightly enlarge the disruption with an instrument used for incising hemorrhoids.
The primary treatment is better when it is proper and not just an incision, and it should not involve drying out unless necessary.
About the instrument, according to its shape:
One part of it is sharp and curved, and the other part is not sharp, with a fine point, but not as thin as a needle, rather slightly blunt.
When the wound is enlarged, and the intestine protrudes, it must be returned to its natural form in its proper place, if possible, as this is better.
But when the disruption is large and in the lower part of the abdomen, make the patient lie on their back with their legs higher than their head; if it is in the upper part of the abdomen, place the patient's head and chest higher than the lower parts.
Similarly, if the disruption is on one side of the abdomen, always ensure that the part with the wound is higher than the other.
And so you should administer this for large and moderate wounds.
For small wounds, use what you have prepared, and ensure the assistant is compassionate, holding the disruption with their hand, adjusting the edges, and revealing the wound to the one performing the suture.
This is the common suturing method: take the needle, or count the needles according to the width of the wound; leave from the end of the disruption on the ring finger, and tighten the needle, making sure not to place the thread in both ends of the suture, with two ends of the linen under the internal parts, until you penetrate from that part; you have already gathered two ends of the suture and two ends of the linen, making four orders.
Then, tighten with double thread around the needle, sometimes from both sides together, and bring the two edges of the wound together properly; leave a finger's width and insert another needle, tying it with thread as you did with the first needle; continue doing this with the needles until you have completed the entire closure of the wound.
The needles should be moderately thick, as very thin needles easily cut the flesh, and very thick ones are difficult to insert into the linen, so they should be of moderate thickness and fineness.
The needle should not be placed too close to the two ends of the linen, to avoid easily cutting the flesh and causing the wound to open before healing.
The sutures should not be prolonged so that the wound is not hindered from healing.
Cut the ends of the needles so they do not hurt the patient while sleeping; apply powders over the sutures to cover the ends of the needles and leave them until you know the wound has healed.
This type of needle suture is more suitable for small wounds, as one or two needles are enough for suturing them.
But the second suture is common: you administer the four ends, two from the linen and two from the flax, at once, with a needle threaded with equally fine and thick thread.
And when you have penetrated these four ends with the needle, return the needle from the same part you started, so the thread retracts from the uppermost part to the wound, making the suture like sewing fabric where the cloth is tightened.
Leave a space between each suture the size of the little finger's thickness.
And this suture is known to people.
And with such a suture, I closed the wound of a man who had been stabbed in the abdomen with a knife. The wound was larger than a palm, and around two palms of the intestine had protruded from the middle part of the abdomen. I reduced it after the intestine had been out of the wound for twenty-four hours.
And the wound healed within about fifteen days. I treated it, and it healed; afterward, he lived for many years and maintained his health.
Other doctors had judged that he would not heal, but it was a miracle because I treated him without ointment, as it was a place where ointment or any medicine was not available. Instead, I applied old cotton twice a day and washed it regularly with honey water until it healed.
But I will describe two specific types of sutures according to Paracelsus.
The first is to start with the suture from the linen, inserting the needle from the outside to the inside.
When the needle penetrates the linen and follows a straight path along the length of the body, leave the end of the thread in which the needle was inserted; then insert the needle in the other end from inside to outside at the other end of the wound.
Once you have done this penetration, insert the needle again from the same end of the wound, from outside to inside; leave the end of the thread on that side and insert the needle on the other end from inside to outside.
Insert the needle with the thread into the end of the wound so that it penetrates completely.
Then, start from the same side and sew it with the end of the thread on the other side. Remove the needle from the linen it penetrated, reinsert it into the linen, and sew the end of the thread on the other side with this end of the wound; remove it from the linen on that side.
Do this once and again, repeating until you have sewn everything outside according to that example.
The essence of this suturing method is to sew it with a stitch similar to leather stitching, sewing the thread from the linen once from the end, and leaving it again until it is completed.
But the second specific type of suture, which is known as Gualia, is described as follows: you sew it similarly to how some doctors sew it, by matching each part to its corresponding part as naturally as possible; and applying the end of one to the end of the wound, as I described to you.
You should place the needle at the end of the wound closest to you from the outside, making it penetrate inside at that point, leaving two ends of the thread; then, turn the needle and make it penetrate from outside to inside at two ends of the thread.
Then, turn each one and make each one penetrate from inside to outside in two other ends of the wound that are on the opposite side.
This method of suturing is better than the common light suture and is the one I mentioned before.
This is the teaching of Gualia.
Some might say that the abdominal suture is done with two needles, each threaded, starting with the insertion of the needle from your side and making it penetrate to the other side, according to the way you would sew skins.
Know that when the disruption is in the middle of the abdomen, suturing it is more difficult than in other parts of the abdomen.
But the assessment of the wound treatment is the same as for other wounds.
The reason is that if you treat the fresh wound with its blood before the air alters it, and you reduce the intestine, sew it, and dress it properly, then you apply an incarnative powder.
If the air has already altered the wound, then apply some ointments that are available to you until it forms a hard scab and the threads fall out, and the skin and flesh are consolidated; then treat it as you would other wounds until it is healed.
But if you don't have medicines available, then apply old cotton as soon as it starts to discharge, changing it twice a day as instructed, until it heals, because you won't need other medication in many cases when the wounds are simple.
But if you fear that the wound might affect the principal organs, then you should soak wool in well-tempered oil or rose oil and apply it around the areas between the groin and under the armpits.
If there is pain or putrefaction in the intestines, which happens often, then wash it with lukewarm astringent black wine, especially if the putrefaction has reached the intestines and is making its way into the abdominal cavity.
And know that a wound in the large intestine is easier to heal than one in the small intestine.
It is difficult to heal because the intestine called "jejunum" does not heal well from any wound due to the large number of veins and the size of those veins, the thinness of its tissue, and its proximity to the nature of the nerves.
But if what protrudes from the wound is the omentum, and you manage it while it is still fresh, reduce it carefully as you would reduce the intestines.
But if it has already turned green or black, then tie it off with thread at the discolored spot to allow blood to flow, because the omentum contains veins and arteries.
Then cut below the tie and leave two ends of the thread hanging from the lower part of the wound outside, to relieve the tension on the omentum and allow for the drainage of the wound.
Recollection of wounds when they occur in the intestines.
When a disruption occurs in the intestines and it is small, it is possible that it can be restored in some people, because I saw someone who had been wounded in the stomach with a lance; the wound was on the right side of the stomach, it had become old and formed a fistula, and there was discharge and gas coming out of it.
So I undertook the treatment of it, even though I was not confident in its healing, but I continued to carefully treat it until it was healed and the area was consolidated.
And once I saw the area consolidated, I feared for the patient that something bad might happen to his stomach; but nothing bad happened, and he remained in good health, eating and drinking, and even taking baths and exercising as he used to.
And some experienced people have said that when there is a wound in the intestine, and it is small, it should be tied in this way: you take ants with large heads; then you bring the two lips of the wound together and place an ant with its mouth open on the two lips of the wound; when it bites and tightens its mouth, you cut off its head, as it adheres and does not come off.
Then place another ant next to the first one, and continue doing this with ants, one next to another, according to the size of the entire wound.
Then you reduce the wound and sew it; and those heads remain attached in the intestine until the intestine is healed; and no harm comes to the patient.
It is also possible that the intestine can be tied with a thin thread made from animal intestines, which is attached to it and then threaded through a needle; you take the end of this gut thread, make it very clean, and tie it to another thread, wrap it, and then insert that thread into the needle and sew the intestine with it, and reduce it in the disruption.
And this method of suturing with ants is only a way of trust and hope.
But if the wound is large and wide, especially if it is in one of the small intestines, then it is not healing but a refinement, and it is not a complete healing.
On the treatment of sores and fistulae.
Know that any wound or abscess, when it worsens and becomes old, and turns into an ulcer, and is not healed and continuously discharges and is not cut off, then, in any part of the body, it is universally called a fistula and we call it azukem.
And a fistula, in truth, is a hard, white, felt-like node that is not painful and has a cavity like a bird's feather shaft; hence, some call it rizatz, that is, feather.
And in some people, it is moist, discharging fluid; and very quickly the moisture can be cut off in a few hours. And sometimes this moisture is abundant, and sometimes it is scant, thick, or thin.
All these causes have been shown, along with their signs and the treatment with medicines, in the division.
And it is necessary that I inform you about the treatment of fistula, which is done by surgical operation.
And know that fistula occurs in all parts of the body; for there are fistulas that reach the large veins and arteries, or the nerves, or the omentum, or the intestines, or the bladder, or some of the ribs, or one of the vertebrae of the back, or some of the joints composed in a way similar to the hand and foot; and among those are the ones that harm the proximity of the principal member and similar areas.
And therefore, those among these fistulas which are of this kind are diseases of difficult healing, and do not receive treatment, and dealing with them is laborious and ignorant.
And if there is a fistula, the depth of which does not reach these places that I mentioned, and you hope and trust in its healing, and want to gain knowledge of it, then take a probe of bronze or iron, and if the fistula goes straight, then explore it with that.
But if the fistula is tortuous, then investigate it with a thin lead probe, because lead, with the lightness of its body, is relieved and curved towards the tortuosity.
And if the fistula has many openings, it is not possible for you to identify it with a probe.
Then inject fluid through its openings, because the moisture with which you inject it will go to the other openings and flow out of them.
And then inquire carefully, in whatever way it is possible for you to determine if there is bone or nerve here, and if the cavity of the fistula is deep or shallow, or if it is a fistula with many openings; consider all this with the sum of your skill, your questioning of the patient, the finding of pain by pressing with your hand on the area, and the appearances of the signs and the causes related to the abscess.
And when you have considered all these causes with true understanding, then come to the treatment with confidence: consider whether the fistula is close or in a safe place, far from a joint, nerve, artery, or vein, or one of the places I mentioned to you.
Then you dry the fistula as previously described, remove what is in it by filtration and the corrupted flesh, and what has grown in the opening from flesh or other similar tissue, and treat it until it is healed.
But if the fistula is deep, and straight, then it must be cut with a knife as much as possible, and then clean it of all its corrupted flesh; and then apply sponges soaked in sharp medicines, and insert them into the cavity of the fistula, which you have understood with the iron probe.
And do this with it several times until the sharp medicine corrodes all that remains in the cavity of the fistula from the corruption.
And then restore it with ointments that generate healthy flesh until it is healed.
But if it is not healed by this, then you cauterize it as we have recommended.
But if the cause of the fistula is bone and you are certain of this, then you examine it and dry it as we have recommended, unless some vein, nerve, or principal part prevents you as we have said.
But if the bone is exposed to you and there is some corruption and blackness, then you scrape it until all the corruption is removed; and then treat it with something that causes it to regenerate until it is healed.
And if it does not heal and still discharges as it did before, then know that you have not completely eradicated the corruption; and then you expose it again and carefully perform the scraping and cleaning with your utmost skill, and then restore it.
And if it is healed, that is good; if not, then know that the corruption is in the deep recesses of the body, which cannot be reached, and then it is best to leave it alone.
And if the corrupted bone is small, or it is possible for you to extract it, then you extract it with fine forceps that are suitable for this.
And if there are many bones, then carefully perform the extraction of all of them, and do not leave any behind with your skill.
But if some cause of moisture interferes with you, and there is no fear or mistake in their incision, then you incise those ligaments or flesh if they are there, and free the bones.
But if it is not the right time for you to remove the bones, then place over the wound something that can rot the wound, and leave it until it reaches around those bones of flesh and ligaments, and keep the wound from healing, and keep the section that you have incised in your operation open.
Further, if you fear, place over it a cotton soaked in soap and oil, or cotton soaked in Egyptian ointment, or in some of the green ointments, for when those ligaments rot, and the bones are separated from them, the removal is eased.
But if it is a large bone such as the bone of the leg or thigh or similar to those, and what is corrupted is only the surface, then scrape it only slightly until that blackness and corruption is removed, and then restore the wound.
But if the corrupted part is large, and the corruption has already reached the marrow of the bone, then it is necessary to close it and cut it all the way to the place where the corruption has reached, and then treat it until it is healed.
And I recount to you what happened to a man in his thigh so that you may take it as an example and help in your operation.
It was a young man about 30 years old, who suffered pain in his thigh, and from the inner parts of the body the bad matter moved to the thigh, and it was matter of an abscess with a large abscess, and it was not caused by an external factor.
And so the time was extended due to the error of the doctors until the abscess burst, and much matter came out, and its treatment was delayed until the leg developed fistulas, and many openings formed, all of which discharged pus and body fluids.
And then many doctors treated him for about two years, and none had a skilled hand in the art, until I was called.
And I saw his leg and the matter flowing from those openings in large amounts; and the man's body was completely wasted and his color was jaundiced.
And then I inserted the probe into one of those openings and the probe reached the bone.
And then I examined all the openings and found that they all reached the bone in various parts of the leg.
And then I hastened and incised over one of those openings until I exposed a part of the bone, and found it corrupted, already crumbly and blackened; and a part of the leg was already corrupted, having penetrated to the marrow.
And then I sawed what was exposed and prepared of the exposed bone.
And I believed that it was not in the bone but only that corruption which I had excised and sawed off, and I had already eradicated it.
And then I applied restorative medicine to the wound for a long time, and it did not heal.
And then I repeated and exposed the bone again from the first exposure, and found continued corruption with the bone.
And I sawed off what appeared to me of that corruption, and then I was forced to treat and restore it, and it did not heal.
And then I exposed it again and did not cease incising the bone little by little in parts and forced its restoration, and it did not heal until I incised about a palm's width of it, and extracted it with its marrow, and then restored it with medicines, and it healed slightly and was healed.
And repetition of the operation and cutting is not necessary except for the condition of its fragility and the lack of its tolerance and fear of death, as it caused frequent bad syncope from excess evacuation.
And then he was healed completely, and in place of the bone there was hard flesh; and his condition was good in his body and his strength returned; and he resumed his usual activities and had no trouble walking nor any other problem.
But if it is a prominent bone in a location of the body that is already cut, then you should proceed in this manner: take a bandage and tie it at the end of the prominent bone, and instruct someone to pull it upwards; place a thicker wool bandage over the first bandage; and then tie it over the flesh below the bone, and extend the end of it so that you pull the flesh downwards, and you expose the flesh over the place you want to saw so that the saw does not harm the flesh.
And place wood or a board under the bone decently, because when you do this the saw will not be prevented from cutting the corrupted flesh.
And the sawing should be done over the corrupted part little by little, because if the corruption is in the cavity of the bone and does not appear on its surface, it will be necessary to saw it again.
And if the bone is corrupted and not prominent, and it is a single continuous piece, and the corruption is in the middle or a part of it, then you expose all the flesh around it; and then place wood under it, and then saw it from the first part where the corruption is until the sawing is continuous with the other part.
And the sawing should be done a little according to the location of the corruption, as we have said.
And if the corruption is in the joint, incise that corrupted joint, and saw the bone where it is continuous with the other part.
And if the corruption is in the continuation of two joints, then it is not in the cutting but rather in the skill.
And if the corruption is in the palm of the hand or the foot, it is very difficult; however, you should examine the corruption in whatever manner it appears to you, and you should expose it, and clean it according to whatever condition is possible for you, and with whatever skill is suitable for you, as long as it does not interfere with any vein or nerve.
And you should know that the cutters and saws used to incise these bones vary according to the location of the bones, their preparation, their thickness, their subtlety, their size, their smallness, their hardness, and their rarity; therefore you must prepare the appropriate instrument for each type of operation.
And you should know that this operation indicates to you the type of instrument you need; because for you there is great study and long experience and knowledge of the methods of this art, and witness to the types of illnesses, as you then have for yourself what is suitable for the instruments for each illness.
And I am providing you at the end of this chapter with the list of instruments that you will use with examples with which you will proceed in your practice which you will experiment with on others. Form of a saw:
